<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Pottery</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Craft of making objects from clay</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the band of the same name, see <a href="/wiki/Pottery_(band)" title="Pottery (band)">Pottery (band)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Potteries_(disambiguation)" title="Potteries (disambiguation)">Potteries (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aufbaukeramik_Wulsttechnik_7.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Aufbaukeramik_Wulsttechnik_7.JPG/330px-Aufbaukeramik_Wulsttechnik_7.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Aufbaukeramik_Wulsttechnik_7.JPG/495px-Aufbaukeramik_Wulsttechnik_7.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Aufbaukeramik_Wulsttechnik_7.JPG/660px-Aufbaukeramik_Wulsttechnik_7.JPG 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aufbaukeramik_Wulsttechnik_7.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shaping and smoothing <a href="/wiki/Fire_clay" title="Fire clay">fire clay</a> to form a <a href="/wiki/Bowl" title="Bowl">bowl</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Pottery</b> is the process and the products of forming vessels and other objects with <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> materials, which are fired at high temperatures to give them a hard and durable form.   Major types include <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">earthenware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoneware" title="Stoneware">stoneware</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>. The place where such wares are made by a <i>potter</i> is also called a <i>pottery</i> (plural "potteries").  The definition of <i>pottery</i>, used by the <a href="/wiki/ASTM_International" title="ASTM International">ASTM International</a>, is "all fired ceramic wares that contain clay when formed, except technical, structural, and refractory products."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In art history and <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, especially of ancient and prehistoric periods, "pottery" often means vessels only, and <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculpted</a> figurines of the same material are called "<a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracottas</a>".  
</p><p>Pottery is one of the <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_historic_inventions" title="Timeline of historic inventions">oldest human inventions</a>, originating before the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic period</a>, with ceramic objects like the <a href="/wiki/Gravettian" title="Gravettian">Gravettian</a> culture <a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Doln%C3%AD_V%C4%9Bstonice" title="Venus of Dolní Věstonice">Venus of Dolní Věstonice</a> figurine discovered in the Czech Republic dating back to 29,000–25,000 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venus_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venus-2">[2]</a></sup> and pottery vessels that were discovered in <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a>, China, which date back to 18,000 BC.  Early Neolithic and pre-Neolithic pottery artifacts have been found, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon" title="Jōmon">Jōmon</a> Japan (10,500 BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jomon_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jomon-3">[3]</a></sup> the Russian Far East (14,000 BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fareastrussia_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-fareastrussia-4">[4]</a></sup> Sub-Saharan Africa (9,400 BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swissinfo_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-swissinfo-5">[5]</a></sup> South America (9,000s–7,000s BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roosevelt_1996_264–349_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roosevelt_1996_264–349-6">[6]</a></sup> and the Middle East (7,000s–6,000s BC).
</p><p>Pottery is made by forming a ceramic (often clay) body into objects of a desired shape and heating them to high temperatures (600–1600 °C) in a <a href="/wiki/Bonfire" title="Bonfire">bonfire</a>, pit or <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kiln</a> and induces reactions that lead to permanent changes including increasing the strength and rigidity of the object.  Much pottery is purely utilitarian, but some can also be regarded as <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_art" title="Ceramic art">ceramic art</a>.  A clay body can be <a href="#Decorating_and_glazing">decorated</a> before or after firing.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_pottery_of_Bengal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1440" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Traditional_pottery_of_Bengal.jpg/220px-Traditional_pottery_of_Bengal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Traditional_pottery_of_Bengal.jpg/330px-Traditional_pottery_of_Bengal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Traditional_pottery_of_Bengal.jpg/440px-Traditional_pottery_of_Bengal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_pottery_of_Bengal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional pottery, Bangladesh</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Niger,_Boubon_(1),_pottery_market.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Niger%2C_Boubon_%281%29%2C_pottery_market.jpg/220px-Niger%2C_Boubon_%281%29%2C_pottery_market.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Niger%2C_Boubon_%281%29%2C_pottery_market.jpg/330px-Niger%2C_Boubon_%281%29%2C_pottery_market.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Niger%2C_Boubon_%281%29%2C_pottery_market.jpg/440px-Niger%2C_Boubon_%281%29%2C_pottery_market.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Niger,_Boubon_(1),_pottery_market.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pottery market in <a href="/wiki/Boubon" title="Boubon">Boubon</a>, Niger</div></div></div>
<p>Clay-based pottery can be divided into three main groups: <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">earthenware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoneware" title="Stoneware">stoneware</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>.  These require increasingly more specific clay material, and increasingly higher firing temperatures. All three are made in <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">glazed</a> and unglazed varieties, for different purposes.  All may also be decorated by various techniques. In many examples the group a piece belongs to is immediately visually apparent, but this is not always the case.  The <a href="/wiki/Fritware" title="Fritware">fritware</a> of the Islamic world does not use clay, so technically falls outside these groups.  Historic pottery of all these types is often grouped as either "fine" wares, relatively expensive and well-made, and following the aesthetic taste of the culture concerned, or alternatively "coarse", "popular", "folk" or "village" wares, mostly undecorated, or simply so, and often less well-made.
</p><p>Cooking in clay pots became less popular once metal pots became available,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">[7]</a></sup> but is still used for dishes that depend on the qualities of pottery cooking, such as <a href="/wiki/Biryani" title="Biryani">biryani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassoulet" title="Cassoulet">cassoulet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daube" title="Daube">daube</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajine" title="Tajine">tagine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jollof_rice" title="Jollof rice">jollof rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kedjenou" title="Kedjenou">kedjenou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cazuela" title="Cazuela">cazuela</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baked_beans" title="Baked beans">baked beans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_types">Main types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Main types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jinan_2009_1145.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1656" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Jinan_2009_1145.jpg/220px-Jinan_2009_1145.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Jinan_2009_1145.jpg/330px-Jinan_2009_1145.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Jinan_2009_1145.jpg/440px-Jinan_2009_1145.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jinan_2009_1145.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">Earthenware</a> pottery from the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> <a href="/wiki/Longshan_culture" title="Longshan culture">Longshan culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, 3rd millennium BC</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earthenware">Earthenware</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Earthenware">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">Earthenware</a></div>
<p>The earliest forms of pottery were made from clays that were fired at low temperatures, initially in pit-fires or in open bonfires. They were hand formed and undecorated. Earthenware can be fired as low as 600 °C, and is normally fired below 1200 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Because unglazed <a href="/wiki/Biscuit_(pottery)" title="Biscuit (pottery)">biscuit</a> earthenware is porous, it has limited utility for the storage of liquids or as tableware. However, earthenware has had a continuous history from the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period to today. It can be made from a wide variety of clays, some of which fire to a buff, brown or black colour, with iron in the constituent minerals resulting in a reddish-brown. Reddish coloured varieties are called <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a>, especially when unglazed or used for sculpture. The development of <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">ceramic glaze</a> made impermeable pottery possible, improving the popularity and practicality of pottery vessels. The addition of decoration has evolved throughout its history.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stoneware">Stoneware</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Stoneware">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stoneware" title="Stoneware">Stoneware</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japan,_Muromachi_period_-_Storage_Jar-_Tamba_Ware_-_2002.66_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7072" data-file-width="6139" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Japan%2C_Muromachi_period_-_Storage_Jar-_Tamba_Ware_-_2002.66_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Japan%2C_Muromachi_period_-_Storage_Jar-_Tamba_Ware_-_2002.66_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Japan%2C_Muromachi_period_-_Storage_Jar-_Tamba_Ware_-_2002.66_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Japan%2C_Muromachi_period_-_Storage_Jar-_Tamba_Ware_-_2002.66_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Japan%2C_Muromachi_period_-_Storage_Jar-_Tamba_Ware_-_2002.66_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Japan%2C_Muromachi_period_-_Storage_Jar-_Tamba_Ware_-_2002.66_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japan,_Muromachi_period_-_Storage_Jar-_Tamba_Ware_-_2002.66_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>15th-century Japanese <a href="/wiki/Stoneware" title="Stoneware">stoneware</a> storage jar, with partial <a href="/wiki/Ash_glaze" title="Ash glaze">ash glaze</a></div></div></div>
<p>Stoneware is pottery that has been fired in a kiln at a relatively high temperature, from about 1,100 °C to 1,200 °C, and is stronger and non-porous to liquids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooper_2010,_p._54_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper_2010,_p._54-9">[9]</a></sup>  The Chinese, who developed stoneware very early on, classify this together with porcelain as high-fired wares.  In contrast, stoneware could only be produced in Europe from the late Middle Ages, as European kilns were less efficient, and the right type of clay less common. It remained a speciality of Germany until the Renaissance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Stoneware is very tough and practical, and much of it has always been utilitarian, for the kitchen or storage rather than the table. But "fine" stoneware has been important in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and the West, and continues to be made.  Many utilitarian types have also come to be appreciated as art.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Porcelain">Porcelain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Porcelain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">Porcelain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chantilly_Porcelain_Factory_-_Teapot_-_1944.228.1_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art_(cropped).tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2080" data-file-width="3572" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Chantilly_Porcelain_Factory_-_Teapot_-_1944.228.1_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Chantilly_Porcelain_Factory_-_Teapot_-_1944.228.1_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Chantilly_Porcelain_Factory_-_Teapot_-_1944.228.1_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Chantilly_Porcelain_Factory_-_Teapot_-_1944.228.1_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Chantilly_Porcelain_Factory_-_Teapot_-_1944.228.1_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Chantilly_Porcelain_Factory_-_Teapot_-_1944.228.1_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chantilly_Porcelain_Factory_-_Teapot_-_1944.228.1_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art_(cropped).tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chantilly_porcelain" title="Chantilly porcelain">Chantilly porcelain</a> teapot, c. 1730, with <a href="/wiki/Chinoiserie" title="Chinoiserie">chinoiserie</a> decoration in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overglaze_enamels" title="Overglaze enamels">overglaze enamels</a></div></div></div>
<p>Porcelain is made by heating materials, generally including <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">kaolin</a>, in a <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kiln</a> to temperatures between 1,200 and 1,400 °C (2,200 and 2,600 °F). This is higher than used for the other types, and achieving these temperatures was a long struggle, as well as realizing what materials were needed. The toughness, strength and translucence of porcelain, relative to other types of pottery, arises mainly from <a href="/wiki/Vitrification#In_ceramics" title="Vitrification">vitrification</a> and the formation of the mineral <a href="/wiki/Mullite" title="Mullite">mullite</a> within the body at these high temperatures.
</p><p>Although porcelain was first made in China, the Chinese traditionally do not recognise it as a distinct category, grouping it with stoneware as "high-fired" ware, opposed to "low-fired" earthenware. This confuses the issue of when it was first made. A degree of translucency and whiteness was achieved by the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (AD 618–906), and considerable quantities were being exported.  The modern level of whiteness was not reached until much later, in the 14th century.  Porcelain was also made in Korea and in Japan from the end of the 16th century, after suitable kaolin was located in those countries. It was not made effectively outside East Asia until the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Archaeology"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology">Archaeology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Archaeology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Working_with_the_pottery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4896" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Working_with_the_pottery.jpg/220px-Working_with_the_pottery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Working_with_the_pottery.jpg/330px-Working_with_the_pottery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Working_with_the_pottery.jpg/440px-Working_with_the_pottery.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Working_with_the_pottery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archaeologist cleaning an early mediaeval pottery sherd from <a href="/wiki/Chodlik" title="Chodlik">Chodlik</a>, Poland.</div></div></div>
<p>The study of pottery can help to provide an insight into past cultures. Fabric analysis (see section below), used to analyse the <i>fabric of pottery</i>, is important part of archaeology for understanding the <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_culture" title="Archaeological culture">archaeological culture</a> of the excavated site by studying the fabric of artifacts, such as their usage, source material composition, decorative pattern, color of patterns, etc. This helps to understand characteristics, sophistication, habits, technology, tools, trade, etc. of the people who made and used the pottery. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dating" title="Carbon dating">Carbon dating</a> reveals the age. Sites with similar pottery characteristics have the same culture, those sites which have distinct cultural characteristics but with some overlap are indicative of cultural exchange such as trade or living in vicinity or continuity of habitation, etc. Examples are <a href="/wiki/Black_and_red_ware" title="Black and red ware">black and red ware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Redware" title="Redware">redware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siswal#Sothi" title="Siswal">Sothi-Siswal culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Painted_Grey_Ware_culture" title="Painted Grey Ware culture">Painted Grey Ware culture</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalibanga#Fabric" title="Kalibanga">six fabrics of Kalibangan</a> is a good example of use of fabric analysis in identifying a differentiated culture which was earlier thought to be typical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a> (IVC) culture.
</p><p>Pottery is durable, and fragments, at least, often survive long after artifacts made from less-durable materials have decayed past recognition. Combined with other evidence, the study of pottery artefacts is helpful in the development of theories on the organisation, economic condition and the cultural development of the societies that produced or acquired pottery. The study of pottery may also allow inferences to be drawn about a culture's daily life, religion, social relationships, attitudes towards neighbours, attitudes to their own world and even the way the culture understood the universe.
</p><p>Chronologies based on pottery are often essential for dating non-literate cultures and are often of help in the dating of historic cultures as well. <a href="/wiki/Elemental_analysis" title="Elemental analysis">Trace-element analysis</a>, mostly by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a>, allows the sources of clay to be accurately identified and the <a href="/wiki/Thermoluminescence" title="Thermoluminescence">thermoluminescence</a> test can be used to provide an estimate of the date of last firing. Examining fired pottery shards from prehistory, scientists learned that during high-temperature firing, iron materials in clay record the exact state of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> at that exact moment.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Source_material">Source material</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Source material">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Fabric"></span><span class="anchor" id="Paste"></span><span class="anchor" id="Fabric_of_clay"></span><span class="anchor" id="Paste_of_clay"></span><span class="anchor" id="Fabric_analysis"></span><span class="anchor" id="Paste_analysis"></span><span class="anchor" id="Pottery_analysis"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fabric_analysis">Fabric analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Fabric analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>"clay body"</i> is also called the <i>"paste"</i> or the <i>"fabric"</i>, which consists of 2 things, the <i>"clay matrix"</i> – composed of grains of less than 0.02 mm grains which can be seen using the high-powered microscopes or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scanning_Electron_Microscope" title="Scanning Electron Microscope">Scanning Electron Microscope</a> (SEM), and the <i>"clay inclusions"</i> – which are larger grains of clay and could be seen with the naked eye or a low-power binocular microscope. For geologists, fabric analysis means spatial arrangement of minerals in a rock. For Archaeologists, the <i>"fabric analysis"</i> of pottery entails the study of <i>clay matrix</i> and <i>inclusions</i> in the clay body as well as the <i>firing temperature and conditions</i>. Analysis is done to examine the following 3 in detail:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fab1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-fab1-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>how pottery was made e.g. material, design such as shape and style, etc.</li>
<li>its decorations, such as patterns, colors of patterns, slipped (glazing) or unslipped decoration</li>
<li>evidence of type of use.</li></ul>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalibanga#Fabric" title="Kalibanga">Six fabrics of Kalibangan</a> is a good example of fabric analysis.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clay_bodies_and_mineral_contents">Clay bodies and mineral contents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Clay bodies and mineral contents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clay_Mixing_for_Pottery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Clay_Mixing_for_Pottery.jpg/220px-Clay_Mixing_for_Pottery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Clay_Mixing_for_Pottery.jpg/330px-Clay_Mixing_for_Pottery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Clay_Mixing_for_Pottery.jpg/440px-Clay_Mixing_for_Pottery.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clay_Mixing_for_Pottery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Preparation of clay for pottery in India</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_(ceramic)" title="Body (ceramic)">Body</a> (or clay body) is a term for the main pottery form of a piece, underneath any glaze or decoration.  The main ingredient of the body is <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a>.  There are several materials that are referred to as clay. The properties which make them different include:
<a href="/wiki/Plasticity_(physics)" title="Plasticity (physics)">Plasticity</a>, the malleability of the body; the extent to which they will absorb water after firing; and shrinkage, the extent of reduction in size of a body as water is removed. Different clay bodies also differ in the way in which they respond when fired in the kiln. A clay body can be <a href="#Decorating_and_glazing">decorated</a> before or after firing. Prior to some shaping processes, clay must be prepared. Each of these different clays is composed of different types and amounts of minerals that determine the characteristics of resulting pottery. There can be regional variations in the properties of raw materials used for the production of pottery, and these can lead to wares that are unique in character to a locality. It is common for clays and other materials to be mixed to produce clay bodies suited to specific purposes. A common component of clay bodies is the mineral <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">kaolinite</a>. Other minerals in the clay, such as <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspar</a>, act as <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_flux" title="Ceramic flux">fluxes</a> which lower the <a href="/wiki/Vitrification#In_ceramics" title="Vitrification">vitrification</a> temperature of bodies. Following is a list of different types of clay used for pottery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaolin" title="Kaolin">Kaolin</a>, is sometimes referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/China_clay" title="China clay">china clay</a> because it was first used in China. Used for <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ball_clay" title="Ball clay">Ball clay</a>: An extremely plastic, fine grained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary" title="Sedimentary">sedimentary</a> clay, which may contain some organic matter. Small amounts can be added to porcelain bodies to increase plasticity.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fire_clay" title="Fire clay">Fire clay</a>: A clay having a slightly lower percentage of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_pottery_terms#F" title="List of pottery terms">fluxes</a> than kaolin, but usually quite plastic. It is highly heat resistant form of clay which can be combined with other clays to increase the firing temperature and may be used as an ingredient to make stoneware type bodies.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stoneware" title="Stoneware">Stoneware</a> <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a>: Suitable for creating stoneware. Has many of the characteristics between fire clay and ball clay, having finer grain, like ball clay but is more heat resistant like fire clays.</li>
<li>Common red clay and <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a> clay have vegetable and ferric oxide impurities which make them useful for bricks, but are generally unsatisfactory for pottery except under special conditions of a particular deposit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bentonite" title="Bentonite">Bentonite</a>: An extremely plastic clay which can be added in small quantities to short clay to increase the plasticity.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_of_pottery">Production of pottery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Production of pottery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Production of pottery includes the following three stages:
</p>
<ul><li>making clay body, i.e. paste or putty.</li>
<li>shaping and moulding</li>
<li>firing or baking</li>
<li>decorating, such as glazing (slipping), painting, etc.</li></ul>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Shaping"></span><span class="anchor" id="Methods"></span><span class="anchor" id="Methods_of_shaping"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shaping_methods">Shaping methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Shaping methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="115" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="PotteryShaping.ogv" height="165" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/PotteryShaping.ogv/220px--PotteryShaping.ogv.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="179304" data-framerate="15" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8e/PotteryShaping.ogv/PotteryShaping.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm;
