<p>Pottery can be shaped by a range of methods that include:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r852221607">.mw-parser-output .flowlist ul{overflow-x:hidden;margin-left:0;padding-left:1.6em}.mw-parser-output .flowlist ol{overflow-x:hidden;margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em}.mw-parser-output .flowlist dl{overflow-x:hidden}</style><div class="flowlist">
<ul><li>Hand-building: This is the earliest forming method. Wares can be constructed by hand from <a href="/wiki/Coiling_(pottery)" title="Coiling (pottery)">coils of clay</a>, combining flat slabs of clay, or <a href="/wiki/Pinch_pot" title="Pinch pot">pinching</a> solid balls of clay or some combination of these. Parts of hand-built vessels are often joined together with the aid of <a href="/wiki/Slip_(ceramics)" title="Slip (ceramics)">slip</a>, an aqueous suspension of clay body and water. A clay body can be <a href="#Decorating_and_glazing">decorated</a> before or after firing. Prior to some shaping processes, clay must be prepared, such as <a href="/wiki/Tableware" title="Tableware">tablewares</a>, although some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studio_potters" title="Studio potters">studio potters</a> find hand-building more conducive to create one-of-a-kind works of <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%B6pferscheibe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1296" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/T%C3%B6pferscheibe.jpg/220px-T%C3%B6pferscheibe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/T%C3%B6pferscheibe.jpg/330px-T%C3%B6pferscheibe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/T%C3%B6pferscheibe.jpg/440px-T%C3%B6pferscheibe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%B6pferscheibe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Classic potter's kick wheel in <a href="/wiki/Erfurt" title="Erfurt">Erfurt</a>, Germany</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electric_potter%27s_wheel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2640" data-file-width="1872" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Electric_potter%27s_wheel.jpg/220px-Electric_potter%27s_wheel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Electric_potter%27s_wheel.jpg/330px-Electric_potter%27s_wheel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Electric_potter%27s_wheel.jpg/440px-Electric_potter%27s_wheel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electric_potter%27s_wheel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An electric potter's wheel, with bat (green disk) and throwing bucket. Not shown is a foot pedal used to control the speed of the wheel, similar to a <a href="/wiki/Sewing_machine" title="Sewing machine">sewing machine</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter's wheel">potter's wheel</a>:  In a process called "throwing" (coming from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> word <i>thrownاا</i> which means to twist or turn,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>) a ball of clay is placed in the center of a turntable, called the wheel-head, which the potter rotates with a stick, with foot power or with a variable-speed <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a>.</li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>During the process of throwing, the wheel rotates while the solid ball of soft clay is pressed, squeezed and pulled gently upwards and outwards into a hollow shape. The first step of pressing the rough ball of clay downward and inward into perfect <a href="/wiki/Rotational_symmetry" title="Rotational symmetry">rotational symmetry</a> is called <i>centring</i> the clay—a most important skill to master before the next steps: <i>opening</i> (making a centred hollow into the solid ball of clay), <i>flooring</i> (making the flat or rounded bottom inside the pot), <i>throwing</i> or <i>pulling</i> (drawing up and shaping the walls to an even thickness), and <i>trimming</i> or <i>turning</i> (removing excess clay to refine the shape or to create a <i>foot</i>).</dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>Considerable skill and experience are required to throw pots of an acceptable standard and, while the ware may have high artistic merit, the reproducibility of the method is poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-16">[16]</a></sup> Because of its inherent limitations, throwing can only be used to create wares with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radial_symmetry" title="Radial symmetry">radial symmetry</a> on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertical_axis" title="Vertical axis">vertical axis</a>. These can then be altered by <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/impression" title="wikt:impression">impressing</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bulge" title="wikt:bulge">bulging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carving" title="Carving">carving</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluting_(architecture)" title="Fluting (architecture)">fluting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cutting" title="Cutting">incising</a>. In addition to the potter's hands these techniques can use tools, including paddles, anvils &amp; ribs, and those specifically for cutting or piercing such as knives, fluting tools, needle tools and wire. Thrown pieces can be further modified by the attachment of handles, lids, feet and spouts.</dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li>Granulate pressing: As the name suggests, this is the operation of shaping pottery by pressing clay in a semi-dry and granulated condition in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moulding_(process)" title="Moulding (process)">mould</a>. The clay is pressed into the mould by a porous die through which water is pumped at high pressure. The <a href="/wiki/Granular_material" title="Granular material">granulated</a> clay is prepared by spray-drying to produce a fine and free-flowing material having a moisture content of between about 5 and 6 per cent. Granulate pressing, also known as <i>dust pressing</i>, is widely used in the manufacture of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceramic_tile" title="Ceramic tile">ceramic tiles</a> and, increasingly, of plates.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Injection_moulding" title="Injection moulding">Injection moulding</a>: This is a shape-forming process adapted for the tableware industry from the method long established for the forming of <a href="/wiki/Thermoplastic" title="Thermoplastic">thermoplastic</a> and some metal components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> It has been called <i>Porcelain Injection Moulding</i>, or <i>PIM</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated103_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated103-18">[18]</a></sup> Suited to the mass production of complex-shaped articles, one significant advantage of the technique is that it allows the production of a <a href="/wiki/Cup" title="Cup">cup</a>, including the handle, in a single process, and thereby eliminates the handle-fixing operation and produces a stronger bond between cup and handle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The feed to the mould die is a mix of approximately 50 to 60 per cent unfired body in powder form, together with 40 to 50 per cent organic additives composed of <a href="/wiki/Binder_(material)" title="Binder (material)">binders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">lubricants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plasticizer" title="Plasticizer">plasticisers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated103_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated103-18">[18]</a></sup> The technique is not as widely used as other shaping methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_pottery_terms#J" title="List of pottery terms">Jiggering and jolleying</a>: These operations are carried out on the potter's wheel and allow the time taken to bring wares to a standardized form to be reduced. <i>Jiggering</i> is the operation of bringing a shaped tool into contact with the plastic clay of a piece under construction, the piece itself being set on a rotating plaster mould on the wheel. The jigger tool shapes one face while the mould shapes the other. Jiggering is used only in the production of flat wares, such as plates, but a similar operation, <i>jolleying</i>, is used in the production of hollow-wares such as cups. Jiggering and jolleying have been used in the production of pottery since at least the 18th century. In large-scale factory production, jiggering and jolleying are usually automated, which allows the operations to be carried out by semi-skilled labour.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3310_-_Athens_-_Sto%C3%A0_of_Attalus_Museum_-_Mould_-_Photo_by_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto,_Nov_9_2009_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1614" data-file-width="1892" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/3310_-_Athens_-_Sto%C3%A0_of_Attalus_Museum_-_Mould_-_Photo_by_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_Nov_9_2009_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-3310_-_Athens_-_Sto%C3%A0_of_Attalus_Museum_-_Mould_-_Photo_by_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_Nov_9_2009_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/3310_-_Athens_-_Sto%C3%A0_of_Attalus_Museum_-_Mould_-_Photo_by_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_Nov_9_2009_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-3310_-_Athens_-_Sto%C3%A0_of_Attalus_Museum_-_Mould_-_Photo_by_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_Nov_9_2009_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/3310_-_Athens_-_Sto%C3%A0_of_Attalus_Museum_-_Mould_-_Photo_by_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_Nov_9_2009_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-3310_-_Athens_-_Sto%C3%A0_of_Attalus_Museum_-_Mould_-_Photo_by_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_Nov_9_2009_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3310_-_Athens_-_Sto%C3%A0_of_Attalus_Museum_-_Mould_-_Photo_by_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto,_Nov_9_2009_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two moulds for terracotta, with modern casts, from ancient Athens, 5–4th centuries BC</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_pottery_terms" title="List of pottery terms">Roller-head machine</a>: This machine is for shaping wares on a rotating mould, as in jiggering and jolleying, but with a rotary shaping tool replacing the fixed profile. The rotary shaping tool is a shallow cone having the same diameter as the ware being formed and shaped to the desired form of the back of the article being made. Wares may in this way be shaped, using relatively unskilled labour, in one operation at a rate of about twelve pieces per minute, though this varies with the size of the articles being produced. Developed in the UK just after World War II by the company <i>Service Engineers</i>, roller-heads were quickly adopted by manufacturers around the world; they remain the dominant method for producing flatware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></li>
<li>Pressure casting: Specially developed polymeric materials allow a mould to be subject to application external pressures of up to 4.0 MPa – so much higher than slip casting in plaster moulds where the capillary forces correspond to a pressure of around 0.1–0.2 MPa. The high pressure leads to much faster casting rates and, hence, faster production cycles. Furthermore, the application of high pressure air through the polymeric moulds upon demoulding the cast means a new casting cycle can be started immediately in the same mould, unlike plaster moulds which require lengthy drying times. The polymeric materials have much greater durability than plaster and, therefore, it is possible to achieve shaped products with better dimensional tolerances and much longer mould life. Pressure casting was developed in the 1970s for the production of sanitaryware although, more recently, it has been applied to tableware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/RAM_press" title="RAM press">RAM pressing</a>: This is used to shape ware by pressing a bat of prepared clay body into a required shape between two porous moulding plates. After pressing, compressed air is blown through the porous mould plates to release the shaped wares.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Slip_casting" title="Slip casting">Slip casting</a>: This is suited to the making of shapes that cannot be formed by other methods. A liquid <a href="/wiki/Slip_(ceramics)" title="Slip (ceramics)">slip</a>, made by mixing <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> body with water, is poured into a highly absorbent plaster mould. Water from the slip is absorbed into the mould leaving a layer of clay body covering its internal surfaces and taking its internal shape. Excess slip is poured out of the mould, which is then split open and the moulded object removed. Slip casting is widely used in the production of sanitaryware and is also used for making other complex shaped ware such as teapots and figurines.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/3D_printing" title="3D printing">3D printing</a>: This is the latest advance in forming ceramic objects. There are two methods. One involves the layered deposition of soft clay similar to fused deposition modeling (FDM) and the other utilizes powder binding techniques where dry clay powder is fused together layer upon layer with a liquid.</li></ul>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Firing">Firing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Firing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pottery_firing_Mali.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Pottery_firing_Mali.jpg/220px-Pottery_firing_Mali.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Pottery_firing_Mali.jpg/330px-Pottery_firing_Mali.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Pottery_firing_Mali.jpg/440px-Pottery_firing_Mali.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pottery_firing_Mali.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pottery firing mound in <a href="/wiki/Kalabougou" title="Kalabougou">Kalabougou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>. All the earliest pottery was made in firing pits of this sort</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bardon_mill_kiln.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Bardon_mill_kiln.jpg/220px-Bardon_mill_kiln.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Bardon_mill_kiln.jpg/330px-Bardon_mill_kiln.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Bardon_mill_kiln.jpg/440px-Bardon_mill_kiln.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bardon_mill_kiln.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kiln</a> at a pottery in <a href="/wiki/Bardon_Mill" title="Bardon Mill">Bardon Mill</a>, UK</div></div></div>
<p>Firing produces irreversible changes in the body. It is only after firing that the article or material is pottery. In lower-fired pottery, the changes include <a href="/wiki/Sintering" title="Sintering">sintering</a>, the fusing together of coarser particles in the body at their points of contact with each other. In the case of porcelain, where different materials and higher firing-temperatures are used, the physical, chemical and mineralogical properties of the constituents in the body are greatly altered. In all cases, the reason for firing is to permanently harden the wares and the firing regime must be appropriate to the materials used to make them. As a rough guide, modern earthenwares are normally fired at temperatures in the range of about 1,000°<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a> (1,830 <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">°F</a>) to 1,200 °C (2,190 °F); stonewares at between about 1,100 °C (2,010 °F)  to 1,300 °C (2,370 °F); and porcelains at between about 1,200 °C (2,190 °F)  to 1,400 °C (2,550 °F). Historically, reaching high temperatures was a long-lasting challenge, and earthenware can be fired effectively as low as 600°<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a>, achievable in primitive <a href="/wiki/Pit_fired_pottery" title="Pit fired pottery">pit firing</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Firing_methods">Firing methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Firing methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Firing pottery can be done using a variety of methods, with a <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kiln</a> being the usual firing method. Both the maximum temperature and the duration of firing influences the final characteristics of the ceramic. Thus, the maximum temperature within a kiln is often held constant for a period of time to <i>soak</i> the wares to produce the maturity required in the body of the wares.
</p><p>The atmosphere within a kiln during firing can affect the appearance of the finished wares. An oxidising atmosphere, produced by allowing an excess of air in the kiln, can cause the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> of clays and glazes. A <a href="/wiki/Reducing_atmosphere" title="Reducing atmosphere">reducing atmosphere</a>, produced by limiting the flow of air into the kiln, or burning coal rather than wood, can strip oxygen from the surface of clays and glazes. This can affect the appearance of the wares being fired and, for example, some glazes containing <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>-rich minerals fire brown in an oxidising atmosphere, but green in a reducing atmosphere. The atmosphere within a kiln can be adjusted to produce complex effects in glaze.
</p><p>Kilns may be heated by burning <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fuel_gas" title="Fuel gas">gas</a>, or by <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. When used as fuels, coal and wood can introduce smoke, soot and ash into the kiln which can affect the appearance of unprotected wares. For this reason, wares fired in wood- or coal-fired kilns are often placed in the kiln in <a href="/wiki/Saggar" title="Saggar">saggars</a>, ceramic boxes, to protect them. Modern kilns powered by gas or electricity are cleaner and more easily controlled than older wood- or coal-fired kilns and often allow shorter firing times to be used. In a Western adaptation of traditional Japanese <a href="/wiki/Raku_ware" title="Raku ware">Raku ware</a> firing, wares are removed from the kiln while hot and smothered in ashes, paper or woodchips which produces a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Carbonization" title="Carbonization">carbonised</a> appearance. This technique is also used in Malaysia in creating traditional <i>labu sayung</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, a firing mound is used rather than a brick or stone kiln. Unfired pots are first brought to the place where a mound will be built, customarily by the women and girls of the village. The mound's foundation is made by placing sticks on the ground, then:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[...] pots are positioned on and amid the branches and then grass is piled high to complete the mound. Although the mound contains the pots of many women, who are related through their husbands' extended families, each women is responsible for her own or her immediate family's pots within the mound.
When a mound is completed and the ground around has been swept clean of residual combustible material, a senior potter lights the fire. A handful of grass is lit and the woman runs around the circumference of the mound touching the burning torch to the dried grass. Some mounds are still being constructed as others are already burning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Firing_stages">Firing stages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Firing stages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Before being shaped, clay must be prepared. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_pottery_terms#K" title="List of pottery terms">Kneading</a> helps to ensure an even moisture content throughout the body. Air trapped within the clay body needs to be removed. This is called de-airing and can be accomplished either by a machine called a vacuum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_pottery_terms#pug" title="List of pottery terms">pug</a> or manually by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_pottery_terms#W" title="List of pottery terms">wedging</a>. Wedging can also help produce an even moisture content. Once a clay body has been kneaded and de-aired or wedged, it is shaped by a variety of techniques. After it has been shaped, it is dried and then fired.
</p>
<ul><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenware_(pottery)" title="Greenware (pottery)">Greenware</a></i> refers to unfired objects. At sufficient moisture content, bodies at this stage are in their most plastic form (as they are soft and malleable, and hence can be easily deformed by handling).</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Leather-hard" title="Leather-hard">Leather-hard</a></i> refers to a clay body that has been dried partially. At this stage the clay object has approximately 15% moisture content. Clay bodies at this stage are very firm and only slightly pliable. Trimming and handle attachment often occurs at the leather-hard state.</li>
<li><i>Bone-dry</i> refers to clay bodies when they reach a moisture content at or near 0%. At that moisture content, the item is ready to be fired.  Additionally, the piece is extremely fragile at this stage and must be handled with extreme care.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Biscuit_(pottery)" title="Biscuit (pottery)">Biscuit</a></i> (or bisque)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> refers to the clay after the object is shaped to the desired form and fired in the kiln for the first time, known as "bisque fired" or "biscuit fired". This firing changes the clay body in several ways. Mineral components of the clay body will undergo chemical and physical changes that will change the material.</li>
<li><i>Glaze fired</i> is the final stage of some pottery making, or <i>glost fired</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-16">[16]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">glaze</a> may be applied to the bisque form and the object can be decorated in several ways. After this the object is "glazed fired", which causes the glaze material to melt, then adhere to the object. Depending on the temperature schedule the glaze firing may also further mature the body as chemical and physical changes continue.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decorating">Decorating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Decorating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CreamBrownCeramicsHidalgo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/CreamBrownCeramicsHidalgo.jpg/220px-CreamBrownCeramicsHidalgo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/CreamBrownCeramicsHidalgo.jpg/330px-CreamBrownCeramicsHidalgo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/CreamBrownCeramicsHidalgo.jpg/440px-CreamBrownCeramicsHidalgo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CreamBrownCeramicsHidalgo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary pottery from the <a href="/wiki/Hidalgo_(state)" title="Hidalgo (state)">State of Hidalgo</a>, Mexico</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Italian_Glazed_Earthenware_Vase-QM_r.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Italian_Glazed_Earthenware_Vase-QM_r.jpg/170px-Italian_Glazed_Earthenware_Vase-QM_r.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Italian_Glazed_Earthenware_Vase-QM_r.jpg/255px-Italian_Glazed_Earthenware_Vase-QM_r.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Italian_Glazed_Earthenware_Vase-QM_r.jpg/340px-Italian_Glazed_Earthenware_Vase-QM_r.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Italian_Glazed_Earthenware_Vase-QM_r.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Italian red earthenware vase covered with a mottled pale blue glaze</div></div></div>
<p>Pottery may be decorated in many different ways. Some decoration can be done before or after the firing.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Decoration_methods">Decoration methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Decoration methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Painting has been used since early prehistoric times (<a href="/wiki/China_painting" title="China painting">China painting</a>), and can be very elaborate. The painting is often applied to pottery that has been fired once, and may then be overlaid with a glaze afterwards. Many <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigments</a> change colour when fired, and the painter must allow for this.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">Glaze</a>: Perhaps the most common form of decoration, that also serves as protection to the pottery, by being tougher and keeping liquid from penetrating the pottery. Glaze may be clear, especially over painting, or coloured and opaque. There is more detail in the section below.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carving" title="Carving">Carving</a>: Pottery vessels may be decorated by shallow carving of the clay body, typically with a knife or similar instrument used on the wheel. This is common in Chinese porcelain of the classic periods.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Burnishing_(pottery)" title="Burnishing (pottery)">Burnishing</a>: The surface of pottery wares may be <i>burnished</i> prior to firing by rubbing with a suitable instrument of wood, steel or stone to produce a polished finish that survives firing. It is possible to produce very highly polished wares when fine clays are used or when the polishing is carried out on wares that have been partially dried and contain little water, though wares in this condition are extremely fragile and the risk of breakage is high.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terra_Sigillata" title="Terra Sigillata">Terra Sigillata</a> is an ancient form of decorating ceramics that was first developed in Ancient Greece.</li>
<li>Additives can be worked into the clay body prior to forming, to produce desired effects in the fired wares. Coarse additives such as sand and <a href="/wiki/Grog_(clay)" title="Grog (clay)">grog</a> (fired clay which has been finely ground) are sometimes used to give the final product a required texture. Contrasting coloured clays and grogs are sometimes used to produce patterns in the finished wares. Colourants, usually metal oxides and carbonates, are added singly or in combination to achieve a desired colour. Combustible particles can be mixed with the body or pressed into the surface to produce texture.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lithography" title="Lithography">Lithography</a>, also called litho, although the alternative names of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transfer-print" title="Transfer-print">transfer print</a> or "<i>decal</i>" are also common. These are used to apply designs to articles. The litho comprises three layers: the colour, or image, layer which comprises the decorative design; the cover coat, a clear protective layer, which may incorporate a low-melting glass; and the backing paper on which the design is printed by screen printing or lithography. There are various methods of transferring the design while removing the backing-paper, some of which are suited to machine application.</li>
<li>Banding is the application by hand or by machine of a band of colour to the edge of a plate or cup. Also known as "lining", this operation is often carried out on a potter's wheel.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Agateware" title="Agateware">Agateware</a> is named after its resemblance to the quartz mineral <a href="/wiki/Agate" title="Agate">agate</a> which has bands or layers of colour that are blended together, agatewares are made by blending clays of differing colours together but not mixing them to the extent that they lose their individual identities. The wares have a distinctive veined or <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mottled" title="wikt:mottled">mottled</a> appearance. The term "agateware" is used to describe such wares in the United Kingdom; in Japan the term "<i>neriage</i>" is used and in China, where such things have been made since at least the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a>, they are called "<i>marbled</i>" wares. Great care is required in the selection of clays to be used for making agatewares as the clays used must have matching thermal movement characteristics.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="278" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/34/Urn.jpg" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An ancient <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenian</a> urn</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/engobe" title="wikt:engobe">Engobe</a>: This is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clay_slip" title="Clay slip">clay slip</a>, that is used to coat the surface of pottery, usually before firing. Its purpose is often decorative though it can also be used to mask undesirable features in the clay to which it is applied. Engobe slip may be applied by painting or by dipping to provide a uniform, smooth, coating. Engobe has been used by potters from pre-historic times until the present day and is sometimes combined with <a href="/wiki/Sgraffito" title="Sgraffito">sgraffito</a> decoration, where a layer of engobe is scratched through to reveal the colour of the underlying clay. With care it is possible to apply a second coat of engobe of a different colour to the first and to incise decoration through the second coat to expose the colour of the underlying coat. Engobes used in this way often contain substantial amounts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a>, sometimes approaching the composition of a <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">glaze</a>.</li>
<li>Gold: Decoration with gold is used on some high quality ware. Different methods exist for its application, including:</li></ul>
<ol><li><i>Best gold</i> – a suspension of gold powder in essential oils mixed with a flux and a mercury salt extended. This can be applied by a painting technique. From the kiln, the decoration is dull and requires burnishing to reveal the full colour</li>
<li><i>Acid Gold</i> – a form of gold decoration developed in the early 1860s at the English factory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mintons_Ltd" title="Mintons Ltd">Mintons Ltd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent" title="Stoke-on-Trent">Stoke-on-Trent</a>. The glazed surface is etched with diluted <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a> prior to application of the gold. The process demands great skill and is used for the decoration only of ware of the highest class.</li>
<li><i>Bright Gold</i> – consists of a solution of gold sulphoresinate together with other metal resonates and a flux. The name derives from the appearance of the decoration immediately after removal from the kiln as it requires no burnishing</li>
<li><i>Mussel Gold</i> – an old method of gold decoration. It was made by rubbing together gold leaf, sugar and salt, followed by washing to remove solubles</li></ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Glazing">Glazing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Glazing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">Ceramic glaze</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:001117_15-44-2002-To-grupper-rosa-Qajar-Fliser2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3462" data-file-width="2733" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/001117_15-44-2002-To-grupper-rosa-Qajar-Fliser2.jpg/200px-001117_15-44-2002-To-grupper-rosa-Qajar-Fliser2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/001117_15-44-2002-To-grupper-rosa-Qajar-Fliser2.jpg/300px-001117_15-44-2002-To-grupper-rosa-Qajar-Fliser2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/001117_15-44-2002-To-grupper-rosa-Qajar-Fliser2.jpg/400px-001117_15-44-2002-To-grupper-rosa-Qajar-Fliser2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:001117_15-44-2002-To-grupper-rosa-Qajar-Fliser2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two panels of <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">earthenware</a> tiles painted with <a href="/wiki/Polychrome" title="Polychrome">polychrome</a> <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">glazes</a> over a white glaze, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, first half of the 19th century.</div></div></div>
<p>Glaze is a glassy coating on pottery, the primary purposes of which are decoration and protection. One important use of glaze is to render porous pottery vessels impermeable to water and other liquids. Glaze may be applied by dusting the unfired composition over the ware or by spraying, dipping, trailing or brushing on a thin <a href="/wiki/Slurry" title="Slurry">slurry</a> composed of the unfired glaze and water. The colour of a glaze after it has been fired may be significantly different from before firing. To prevent glazed wares sticking to <a href="/wiki/Kiln_furniture" title="Kiln furniture">kiln furniture</a> during firing, either a small part of the object being fired (for example, the foot) is left unglazed or, alternatively, special refractory "<i>spurs</i>" are used as supports. These are removed and discarded after the firing.
</p><p>Some specialised glazing techniques include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Salt_glaze_pottery" title="Salt glaze pottery">Salt-glazing</a>, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_salt" title="Common salt">common salt</a> is introduced to the kiln during the firing process. The high temperatures cause the salt to volatize, depositing it on the surface of the ware to react with the body to form a sodium aluminosilicate glaze. In the 17th and 18th centuries, salt-glazing was used in the manufacture of domestic pottery. Now, except for use by some studio potters, the process is obsolete. The last large-scale application before its demise in the face of environmental clean air restrictions was in the production of salt-glazed <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_sewer" title="Sanitary sewer">sewer-pipes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ash_glaze" title="Ash glaze">Ash glazing</a> – ash from the combustion of plant matter has been used as the flux component of glazes. The source of the ash was generally the combustion waste from the fuelling of kilns although the potential of ash derived from arable crop wastes has been investigated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Ash glazes are of historical interest in the Far East although there are reports of small-scale use in other locations such as the <a href="/wiki/Catawba_Valley_Pottery" title="Catawba Valley Pottery">Catawba Valley Pottery</a> in the United States. They are now limited to small numbers of studio potters who value the unpredictability arising from the variable nature of the raw material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Underglaze" title="Underglaze">Underglaze</a> decoration (in the manner of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_and_white_porcelain" title="Blue and white porcelain">blue and white wares</a>). Underglaze may be applied by brush strokes, air brush, or by pouring the underglaze into the mould, covering the inside, creating a swirling effect, then the mould is filled with slip.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/In-glaze_decoration" title="In-glaze decoration">In-glaze decoration</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On-glaze_decoration" title="On-glaze decoration">On-glaze decoration</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enamelling" title="Enamelling">Enamel</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_art#History" title="Ceramic art">Ceramic art § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vestonicka_venuse_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1992" data-file-width="1020" decoding="async" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vestonicka_venuse_edit.jpg/170px-Vestonicka_venuse_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vestonicka_venuse_edit.jpg/255px-Vestonicka_venuse_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vestonicka_venuse_edit.jpg/340px-Vestonicka_venuse_edit.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vestonicka_venuse_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earliest known ceramics are the <a href="/wiki/Gravettian" title="Gravettian">Gravettian</a> figurines that date to 29,000 to 25,000 BC.</div></div></div>
<p>A great part of the history of pottery is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a>, part of past pre-literate cultures. Therefore, much of this history can only be found among the <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">artifacts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>. Because pottery is so durable, pottery and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sherd" title="Sherd">shards</a> of pottery survive from millennia at <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_site" title="Archaeological site">archaeological sites</a>, and are typically the most common and important type of artifact to survive.  Many prehistoric cultures are named after the pottery that is the easiest way to identify their sites, and archaeologists develop the ability to recognise different types from the chemistry of small shards.
</p><p>Before pottery becomes part of a culture, several conditions must generally be met.
</p>
<ul><li>First, there must be usable clay available. Archaeological sites where the earliest pottery was found were near deposits of readily available clay that could be properly shaped and fired. China has large deposits of a variety of clays, which gave them an advantage in early development of fine pottery. Many countries have large deposits of a variety of clays.</li>
<li>Second, it must be possible to heat the pottery to temperatures that will achieve the transformation from raw clay to ceramic. Methods to reliably create fires hot enough to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pottery_firing" title="Pottery firing">fire pottery</a> did not develop until late in the development of cultures.</li>
<li>Third, the potter must have time available to prepare, shape and fire the clay into pottery. Even after control of fire was achieved, humans did not seem to develop pottery until a <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentary life</a> was achieved. It has been hypothesized that pottery was developed only after humans established agriculture, which led to permanent settlements. However, the <a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Doln%C3%AD_V%C4%9Bstonice" title="Venus of Dolní Věstonice">oldest known pottery</a> is from the Czech Republic and dates to 28,000 BC, at the height of the ice age, long before the beginnings of agriculture.</li>
<li>Fourth, there must be a sufficient need for pottery in order to justify the resources required for its production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JomonPottery.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="524" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/JomonPottery.JPG/170px-JomonPottery.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/JomonPottery.JPG/255px-JomonPottery.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/JomonPottery.JPG/340px-JomonPottery.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JomonPottery.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Incipient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon" title="Jōmon">Jōmon</a> pottery vessel reconstructed from fragments (10,000–8,000 BC), <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum" title="Tokyo National Museum">Tokyo National Museum</a>, Japan</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_pottery">Early pottery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Early pottery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Methods of forming: Hand-shaping was the earliest method used to form vessels. This included the combination of <a href="/wiki/Pinch_pot" title="Pinch pot">pinching</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Coiling_(pottery)" title="Coiling (pottery)">coiling</a>.</li>
<li>Firing: The earliest method for firing pottery wares was the use of bonfires <a href="/wiki/Pit_fired_pottery" title="Pit fired pottery">pit fired pottery</a>. Firing times might be short but the peak-temperatures achieved in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pottery_firing" title="Pottery firing">fire</a> could be high, perhaps in the region of 900 °C (1,650 °F), and were reached very quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li>Clay: Early potters used whatever clay was available to them in their geographic vicinity. However, the lowest quality common red clay was adequate for low-temperature fires used for the earliest pots. Clays <a href="/wiki/Temper_(pottery)" title="Temper (pottery)">tempered</a> with sand, grit, crushed shell or crushed pottery were often used to make bonfire-fired ceramics because they provided an open-body texture that allowed water and volatile components of the clay to escape freely. The coarser particles in the clay also acted to restrain shrinkage during drying, and hence reduce the risk of cracking.</li>
<li>Form: In the main, early bonfire-fired wares were made with rounded bottoms to avoid sharp angles that might be susceptible to cracking.</li>
<li>Glazing: the earliest pots were not glazed.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter's wheel">potter's wheel</a> was invented in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> sometime between 6,000 and 4,000 BC (<a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a>) and revolutionised pottery production.</li>
<li>Moulds were used to a limited extent as early as the 5th and 6th century BC by the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_civilization" title="Etruscan civilization">Etruscans</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and more extensively by the Romans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slipcasting" title="Slipcasting">Slipcasting</a>, a popular method for shaping irregular shaped articles. It was first practised, to a limited extent, in China as early as the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li>Transition to kilns: The earliest intentionally constructed were <a href="/wiki/Pit_fired_pottery" title="Pit fired pottery">pit-kilns</a> or trench-kilns—holes dug in the ground and covered with fuel. Holes in the ground provided insulation and resulted in better control over firing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kiln#Ceramic_kilns" title="Kiln">Kilns</a>: Pit fire methods were adequate for creating simple earthenware, but other pottery types needed more sophisticated kilns (see below <a href="#Kilns">kilns</a>).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_by_region">History by region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: History by region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginnings_of_pottery">Beginnings of pottery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Beginnings of pottery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Xianrendong_Cave_Pottery_-_2.jpg/276px-Xianrendong_Cave_Pottery_-_2.jpg 2x" width="138"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:198px;max-width:198px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:148px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pottery_fragment_Xianrendong_cave.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="194" data-file-width="255" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Pottery_fragment_Xianrendong_cave.png/196px-Pottery_fragment_Xianrendong_cave.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/94/Pottery_fragment_Xianrendong_cave.png 1.5x" width="196"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flow-root"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Xianren_Cave" title="Xianren Cave">Xianren Cave</a> pottery fragments, radiocarbon dated to circa 18,000 BC, China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pottery_bowl,_7100-5800_BCE,_from_Jarmo,_Sulaymaniyah,_Iraq._Sulaymaniyah_Museum,_Iraqi_Kurdistan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2275" data-file-width="2984" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pottery_bowl%2C_7100-5800_BCE%2C_from_Jarmo%2C_Sulaymaniyah%2C_Iraq._Sulaymaniyah_Museum%2C_Iraqi_Kurdistan.jpg/220px-Pottery_bowl%2C_7100-5800_BCE%2C_from_Jarmo%2C_Sulaymaniyah%2C_Iraq._Sulaymaniyah_Museum%2C_Iraqi_Kurdistan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pottery_bowl%2C_7100-5800_BCE%2C_from_Jarmo%2C_Sulaymaniyah%2C_Iraq._Sulaymaniyah_Museum%2C_Iraqi_Kurdistan.jpg/330px-Pottery_bowl%2C_7100-5800_BCE%2C_from_Jarmo%2C_Sulaymaniyah%2C_Iraq._Sulaymaniyah_Museum%2C_Iraqi_Kurdistan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pottery_bowl%2C_7100-5800_BCE%2C_from_Jarmo%2C_Sulaymaniyah%2C_Iraq._Sulaymaniyah_Museum%2C_Iraqi_Kurdistan.jpg/440px-Pottery_bowl%2C_7100-5800_BCE%2C_from_Jarmo%2C_Sulaymaniyah%2C_Iraq._Sulaymaniyah_Museum%2C_Iraqi_Kurdistan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pottery_bowl,_7100-5800_BCE,_from_Jarmo,_Sulaymaniyah,_Iraq._Sulaymaniyah_Museum,_Iraqi_Kurdistan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pottery bowl from <a href="/wiki/Jarmo" title="Jarmo">Jarmo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, 7100–5800 BC.</div></div></div>
<p>Pottery may well have been discovered independently in various places, probably by accidentally creating it at the bottom of fires on a clay soil.  All the earliest vessel forms were <a href="/wiki/Pit_fired_pottery" title="Pit fired pottery">pit fired</a> and made by <a href="/wiki/Coiling_(pottery)" title="Coiling (pottery)">coiling</a>, which is a simple technology to learn.  The earliest-known ceramic objects are <a href="/wiki/Gravettian" title="Gravettian">Gravettian</a> figurines such as those discovered at Dolní Věstonice in the modern-day Czech Republic. The <a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Doln%C3%AD_V%C4%9Bstonice" title="Venus of Dolní Věstonice">Venus of Dolní Věstonice</a> is a Venus figurine, a statuette of a nude female figure dated to 29,000–25,000 BC (Gravettian industry).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venus_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Venus-2">[2]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sherd" title="Sherd">Sherds</a> have been found in China and Japan from a period between 12,000 and perhaps as long as 18,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fareastrussia_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-fareastrussia-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cl_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-cl-43">[43]</a></sup> As of 2012, the earliest pottery found anywhere in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> dating to 20,000 to 19,000 years before the present, was found at <a href="/wiki/Xianren_Cave" title="Xianren Cave">Xianrendong Cave</a> in the Jiangxi province of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xianrendong_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xianrendong-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other early pottery vessels include those excavated from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuchanyan_Cave" title="Yuchanyan Cave">Yuchanyan Cave</a> in southern China, dated from 16,000 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cl_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-cl-43">[43]</a></sup> and those found in the Amur River basin in the Russian Far East, dated from 14,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fareastrussia_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-fareastrussia-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odai_Yamamoto_I_site" title="Odai Yamamoto I site">Odai Yamamoto I site</a>, belonging to the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="Jōmon period">Jōmon period</a>, currently has the oldest pottery in Japan. Excavations in 1998 uncovered <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">earthenware</a> fragments which have been dated as early as 14,500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
The term "Jōmon" means "cord-marked" in Japanese. This refers to the markings made on the vessels and figures using sticks with cords during their production. Recent research has elucidated how <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_pottery" title="Jōmon pottery">Jōmon pottery</a> was used by its creators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earliest_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Earliest-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>It appears that pottery was independently developed in Sub-Saharan Africa during the 10th millennium BC, with findings dating to at least 9,400 BC from central <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swissinfo_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-swissinfo-5">[5]</a></sup> and in South America during the 9,000s–7,000s BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roosevelt_1996_264–349_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roosevelt_1996_264–349-6">[6]</a></sup> The Malian finds date to the same period as similar finds from East Asia – the triangle between Siberia, China and Japan – and are associated in both regions to the same climatic changes (at the end of the ice age new grassland develops, enabling hunter-gatherers to expand their habitat), met independently by both cultures with similar developments: the creation of pottery for the storage of wild cereals (<a href="/wiki/Pearl_millet" title="Pearl millet">pearl millet</a>), and that of small arrowheads for hunting small game typical of grassland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swissinfo_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-swissinfo-5">[5]</a></sup> Alternatively, the creation of pottery in the case of the Incipient Jōmon civilisation could be due to the intensive exploitation of freshwater and marine organisms by late glacial foragers, who started developing ceramic containers for their catch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earliest_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Earliest-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asia">East Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: East Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramics" title="Chinese ceramics">Chinese ceramics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_pottery" title="Korean pottery">Korean pottery</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_pottery" title="Japanese pottery">Japanese pottery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ming-Schale1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="583" data-file-width="709" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Ming-Schale1.jpg/220px-Ming-Schale1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Ming-Schale1.jpg/330px-Ming-Schale1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Ming-Schale1.jpg/440px-Ming-Schale1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ming-Schale1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese Ming dynasty <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue-and-white_porcelain" title="Blue-and-white porcelain">blue-and-white porcelain</a> dish with a dragon</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1620" data-file-width="2838" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg/220px-Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg/330px-Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg/440px-Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%A8s_chinois_Guimet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Group of 13th-century pieces of <a href="/wiki/Longquan_celadon" title="Longquan celadon">Longquan celadon</a></div></div></div>
<p>In Japan, the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="Jōmon period">Jōmon period</a> has a long history of development of <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_pottery" title="Jōmon pottery">Jōmon pottery</a> which was characterized by impressions of rope on the surface of the pottery created by pressing rope into the clay before firing. Glazed Stoneware was being created as early as the 15th century BC in China. A form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_porcelain" title="Chinese porcelain">Chinese porcelain</a> became a significant Chinese export from the Tang Dynasty (AD 618–906) onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooper_2010,_p._54_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper_2010,_p._54-9">[9]</a></sup>  Korean potters adopted porcelain as early as the 14th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Japanese porcelain was made in the early 16th century after Shonzui Goradoyu-go brought back the secret of its manufacture from the Chinese kilns at Jingdezhen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast to Europe, the Chinese elite used pottery extensively at table, for religious purposes, and for decoration, and the standards of fine pottery were very high. From the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960–1279) for several centuries elite taste favoured plain-coloured and exquisitely formed pieces; during this period true porcelain was perfected in <a href="/wiki/Ding_ware" title="Ding ware">Ding ware</a>, although it was the only one of the <a href="/wiki/Five_Great_Kilns" title="Five Great Kilns">Five Great Kilns</a> of the Song period to use it. The traditional Chinese category of high-fired wares includes stoneware types such as <a href="/wiki/Ru_ware" title="Ru ware">Ru ware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Longquan_celadon" title="Longquan celadon">Longquan celadon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guan_ware" title="Guan ware">Guan ware</a>.  Painted wares such as <a href="/wiki/Cizhou_ware" title="Cizhou ware">Cizhou ware</a> had a lower status, though they were acceptable for making pillows.
</p><p>The arrival of Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_and_white_porcelain" title="Blue and white porcelain">blue and white porcelain</a> was probably a product of the Mongol <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> (1271–1368) dispersing artists and craftsmen across its large empire. Both the <a href="/wiki/Cobalt(II)_oxide" title="Cobalt(II) oxide">cobalt</a> stains used for the blue colour, and the style of painted decoration, usually based on plant shapes, were initially borrowed from the Islamic world, which the Mongols had also conquered. At the same time <a href="/wiki/Jingdezhen_porcelain" title="Jingdezhen porcelain">Jingdezhen porcelain</a>, produced in Imperial factories, took the undisputed leading role in production, which it has retained to the present day. The new elaborately painted style was now favoured at court, and gradually more colours were added.
</p><p>The secret of making such porcelain was sought in the Islamic world and later in Europe when examples were imported from the East. Many attempts were made to imitate it in Italy and France. However it was not produced outside of the Orient until 1709 in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asia">South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pottery_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Pottery in the Indian subcontinent">Pottery in the Indian subcontinent</a></div>
<p>Cord-Impressed style pottery belongs to 'Mesolithic' ceramic tradition that developed among Vindhya hunter-gatherers in Central India during the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> This ceramic style is also found in later Proto-Neolithic phase in nearby regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> This early type of pottery, also found at the site of <a href="/wiki/Lahuradewa" title="Lahuradewa">Lahuradewa</a>, is currently the oldest known pottery tradition in South Asia, dating back to 7,000–6,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Wheel-made pottery began to be made during the <a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh</a> Period II (5,500–4,800 BC) and Merhgarh Period III (4,800–3,500 BC), known as the ceramic Neolithic and <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">chalcolithic</a>. Pottery, including items known as the ed-Dur vessels, originated in regions of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Saraswati River / Indus River</a> and have been found in a number of sites in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Civilization" title="Indus Civilization">Indus Civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite an extensive prehistoric record of pottery, including painted wares, little "fine" or luxury pottery was made in the subcontinent in historic times. <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> discourages eating off pottery, which probably largely accounts for this.  Most traditional Indian pottery vessels are large pots or jars for storage, or small cups or lamps, often treated as disposable.  In contrast there are long traditions of sculpted figures, often rather large, in terracotta.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
