<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Philippine_ceramics" title="Philippine ceramics">Philippine ceramics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manunggul_Jar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1980" data-file-width="1762" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Manunggul_Jar.jpg/220px-Manunggul_Jar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Manunggul_Jar.jpg/330px-Manunggul_Jar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Manunggul_Jar.jpg/440px-Manunggul_Jar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manunggul_Jar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Late_Neolithic" title="Late Neolithic">Late Neolithic</a> <a href="/wiki/Manunggul_Jar" title="Manunggul Jar">Manunggul Jar</a> from <a href="/wiki/Palawan" title="Palawan">Palawan</a> used for burial, topped with two figures representing the journey of the soul into the afterlife.</div></div></div>
<p>Pottery in Southeast Asia is as diverse as its ethnic groups. Each ethnic group has their own set of standards when it comes to pottery arts. Potteries are made due to various reasons, such as trade, food and beverage storage, kitchen usage, religious ceremonies, and burial purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Asia">West Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: West Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Levantine_pottery" title="Levantine pottery">Levantine pottery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_pottery" title="Persian pottery">Persian pottery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pottery_of_ancient_Cyprus" title="Pottery of ancient Cyprus">Pottery of ancient Cyprus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jar_MET_VS1985_356_16.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="708" data-file-width="835" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Jar_MET_VS1985_356_16.jpg/220px-Jar_MET_VS1985_356_16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Jar_MET_VS1985_356_16.jpg/330px-Jar_MET_VS1985_356_16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Jar_MET_VS1985_356_16.jpg/440px-Jar_MET_VS1985_356_16.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jar_MET_VS1985_356_16.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Before the invention of pottery in Western Asia, during the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic">Pre-Pottery Neolithic</a>: jar in calcite <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a>, Syria, late 8th millennium BC.</div></div></div>
<p>Around 8000 BC during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-pottery_Neolithic" title="Pre-pottery Neolithic">Pre-pottery Neolithic</a> period, and before the invention of pottery, several early settlements became experts in crafting beautiful and highly sophisticated containers from stone, using materials such as <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a> or <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a>, and employing sand to shape and polish. Artisans used the veins in the material to maximum visual effect. Such objects have been found in abundance on the upper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euphrates_river" title="Euphrates river">Euphrates river</a>, in what is today eastern Syria, especially at the site of <a href="/wiki/Bouqras" title="Bouqras">Bouqras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest history of pottery production in the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> starts the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pottery_Neolithic" title="Pottery Neolithic">Pottery Neolithic</a> and can be divided into four periods, namely: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hassuna" title="Hassuna">Hassuna</a> period (7000–6500 BC), the Halaf period (6500–5500 BC), the <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a> (5500–4000 BC), and the Uruk period (4000–3100 BC).  By about 5000 BC pottery-making was becoming widespread across the region, and spreading out from it to neighbouring areas.
</p><p>Pottery making began in the 7th millennium BC. The earliest forms, which were found at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hassuna" title="Hassuna">Hassuna</a> site, were hand formed from slabs, undecorated, unglazed low-fired pots made from reddish-brown clays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-40">[40]</a></sup>  Within the next millennium, wares were decorated with elaborate painted designs and natural forms, incising and burnished.
</p><p>The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter's wheel">potter's wheel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> sometime between 6000 and 4000 BC (<a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a>) revolutionized pottery production. Newer kiln designs could fire wares to 1,050 °C (1,920 °F) to 1,200 °C (2,190 °F) which enabled new possibilities and new preparation of clays. Production was now carried out by small groups of potters for small cities, rather than individuals making wares for a family. The shapes and range of uses for ceramics and pottery expanded beyond simple vessels to store and carry to specialized cooking utensils, pot stands and rat traps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> As the region developed, new organizations and political forms, pottery became more elaborate and varied. Some wares were made using moulds, allowing for increased production for the needs of the growing populations. Glazing was commonly used and pottery was more decorated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> period in Mesopotamia, <a href="/wiki/Halaf_culture#Halaf_pottery" title="Halaf culture">Halafian pottery</a> achieved a level of technical competence and sophistication, not seen until the later developments of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_pottery" title="Greek pottery">Greek pottery</a> with Corinthian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attic_ware" title="Attic ware">Attic ware</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Minoan_pottery" title="Minoan pottery">Minoan pottery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pottery_of_ancient_Greece" title="Pottery of ancient Greece">Pottery of ancient Greece</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_pottery" title="Ancient Roman pottery">Ancient Roman pottery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Altamura_Painter_-_Red-Figure_Calyx_Krater_-_Walters_48262_-_Side_A.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1799" data-file-width="1652" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Altamura_Painter_-_Red-Figure_Calyx_Krater_-_Walters_48262_-_Side_A.jpg/220px-Altamura_Painter_-_Red-Figure_Calyx_Krater_-_Walters_48262_-_Side_A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Altamura_Painter_-_Red-Figure_Calyx_Krater_-_Walters_48262_-_Side_A.jpg/330px-Altamura_Painter_-_Red-Figure_Calyx_Krater_-_Walters_48262_-_Side_A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Altamura_Painter_-_Red-Figure_Calyx_Krater_-_Walters_48262_-_Side_A.jpg/440px-Altamura_Painter_-_Red-Figure_Calyx_Krater_-_Walters_48262_-_Side_A.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Altamura_Painter_-_Red-Figure_Calyx_Krater_-_Walters_48262_-_Side_A.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greek <a href="/wiki/Red-figure_pottery" title="Red-figure pottery">red-figure</a> vase in the <a href="/wiki/Krater" title="Krater">krater</a> shape, between 470 and 460 BC, by the <a href="/wiki/Altamura_Painter" title="Altamura Painter">Altamura Painter</a></div></div></div>
<p>Europe's oldest pottery, dating from circa 6700 BC, was found on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Samara_(Volga)" title="Samara (Volga)">Samara River</a> in the middle <a href="/wiki/Volga" title="Volga">Volga</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> These sites are known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yelshanka_culture" title="Yelshanka culture">Yelshanka culture</a>.
</p><p>The early inhabitants of Europe developed pottery in the <a href="/wiki/Linear_Pottery_culture" title="Linear Pottery culture">Linear Pottery culture</a> slightly later than the Near East, circa 5500–4500 BC. In the ancient Western Mediterranean elaborately painted earthenware reached very high levels of artistic achievement in the Greek world; there are large numbers of survivals from tombs.  Minoan pottery was characterized by complex painted decoration with natural themes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>  The classical Greek culture began to emerge around 1000 BC featuring a variety of well crafted pottery which now included the human form as a decorating motif. The pottery wheel was now in regular use. Although glazing was known to these potters, it was not widely used. Instead, a more porous clay slip was used for decoration. A wide <a href="/wiki/Typology_of_Greek_vase_shapes" title="Typology of Greek vase shapes">range of shapes</a> for different uses developed early and remained essentially unchanged during Greek history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscan_pottery" title="Etruscan pottery">Etruscan pottery</a> was heavily influenced by Greek pottery and often imported Greek potters and painters. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_pottery" title="Ancient Roman pottery">Ancient Roman pottery</a> made much less use of painting, but used moulded decoration, allowing industrialized production on a huge scale.  Much of the so-called red <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samian_ware" title="Samian ware">Samian ware</a> of the Early <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> was in fact produced in modern Germany and France, where entrepreneurs established large potteries.  Excavations at <a href="/wiki/Augusta_Raurica" title="Augusta Raurica">Augusta Raurica</a>, near Basel, Switzerland, have revealed a pottery production site in use from the 1st to the 4th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pottery was hardly seen on the tables of elites from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> times until the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, and most medieval wares were coarse and utilitarian, as the elites ate off metal vessels.  Painted <a href="/wiki/Hispano-Moresque_ware" title="Hispano-Moresque ware">Hispano-Moresque ware</a> from Spain, developing the styles of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_Spain" title="Islamic Spain">Islamic Spain</a>, became a luxury for late medieval elites, and was adapted in Italy into <i><a href="/wiki/Maiolica" title="Maiolica">maiolica</a></i> in the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>.  Both of these were <a href="/wiki/Faience" title="Faience">faience</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin-glazed" title="Tin-glazed">tin-glazed</a> earthenware, and fine faience continued to be made until around 1800 in various countries, especially France, with <a href="/wiki/Nevers_faience" title="Nevers faience">Nevers faience</a> and several other centres.  In the 17th century, imports of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_export_porcelain" title="Chinese export porcelain">Chinese export porcelain</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Japanese_export_porcelain" title="Japanese export porcelain">Japanese equivalent</a> raised the market 
expectations of fine pottery, and European manufacturers eventually learned to make porcelain, often in the form of "artificial" or <a href="/wiki/Soft-paste_porcelain" title="Soft-paste porcelain">soft-paste porcelain</a>, and from the 18th century European porcelain and other wares from a great number of producers became extremely popular, reducing Asian imports.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wedgwood" title="Wedgwood">Wedgwood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire_figure" title="Staffordshire figure">Staffordshire figure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Doulton" title="Royal Doulton">Royal Doulton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mintons" title="Mintons">Mintons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midwinter_Pottery" title="Midwinter Pottery">Midwinter Pottery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spode" title="Spode">Spode</a></div>
<p>The English city of <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent" title="Stoke-on-Trent">Stoke-on-Trent</a> is widely known as "The Potteries" because of the large number of pottery factories or, colloquially, "Pot Banks". It was one of the first industrial cities of the modern era where, as early as 1785, two hundred pottery manufacturers employed 20,000 workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Wedgwood" title="Josiah Wedgwood">Josiah Wedgwood</a> (1730–1795) was the dominant leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>In North Staffordshire hundreds of companies produced all kinds of pottery, from tablewares and decorative pieces to industrial items.  The main pottery types of earthenware, stoneware and porcelain were all made in large quantities, and the Staffordshire industry was a major innovator in developing new varieties of ceramic bodies such as <a href="/wiki/Bone_china" title="Bone china">bone china</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jasperware" title="Jasperware">jasperware</a>, as well as pioneering <a href="/wiki/Transfer_printing" title="Transfer printing">transfer printing</a> and other glazing and decorating techniques.  In general Staffordshire was strongest in the middle and low price ranges, though the finest and most expensive types of wares were also made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late 18th century North Staffordshire was the largest producer of ceramics in Britain, despite significant centres elsewhere.  Large export markets took Staffordshire pottery around the world, especially in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>  Production had begun to decline in the late 19th century, as other countries developed their industries, and declined steeply after World War II.  Some production continues in the area, but at a fraction of the levels at the peak of the industry.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_pottery">Islamic pottery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Islamic pottery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_pottery" title="Islamic pottery">Islamic pottery</a></div>
<p>Early <a href="/wiki/Islamic_pottery" title="Islamic pottery">Islamic pottery</a> followed the forms of the regions which the Muslims conquered. Eventually, however, there was cross-fertilization between the regions. This was most notable in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_influences_on_Islamic_pottery" title="Chinese influences on Islamic pottery">Chinese influences on Islamic pottery</a>. Trade between China and Islam took place via the system of trading posts over the lengthy <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>. Islamic nations imported stoneware and later porcelain from China. China imported the minerals for <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">Cobalt blue</a> from the Islamic ruled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> to decorate their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_and_white_porcelain" title="Blue and white porcelain">blue and white porcelain</a>, which they then exported to the Islamic world.
</p><p>Likewise, Islamic art contributed to a lasting pottery form identified as  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hispano-Moresque" title="Hispano-Moresque">Hispano-Moresque</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andalucia" title="Andalucia">Andalucia</a> (Islamic Spain). Unique Islamic forms were also developed, including <a href="/wiki/Fritware" title="Fritware">fritware</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lusterware" title="Lusterware">lusterware</a> and specialized glazes like <a href="/wiki/Tin-glazing" title="Tin-glazing">tin-glazing</a>, which led to the development of the popular <a href="/wiki/Maiolica" title="Maiolica">maiolica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>One major emphasis in ceramic development in the Muslim world was the use of <a href="/wiki/Tile" title="Tile">tile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tile#Decorative_tilework_and_coloured_brick" title="Tile">decorative tilework</a>.
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ceramics_of_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Ceramics of indigenous peoples of the Americas">Ceramics of indigenous peoples of the Americas</a></div>
<p>Most evidence points to an independent development of pottery in the Native American cultures, with the earliest known dates from Brazil, from 9,500 to 5,000 years ago and 7,000 to 6,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roosevelt_1996_264–349_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Roosevelt_1996_264–349-6">[6]</a></sup> Further north in <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, dates begin with the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology#archaic" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Archaic Era</a> (3500–2000 BC), and into the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology#PreclaPreclassic_Era_or_Formative_Period" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Formative period</a> (2000 BC – AD 200). These cultures did not develop the stoneware, porcelain or glazes found in the Old World.  <a href="/wiki/Maya_ceramics" title="Maya ceramics">Maya ceramics</a> include finely painted vessels, usually beakers, with elaborate scenes with several figures and texts.  Several cultures, beginning with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmec</a>, made terracotta sculpture, and sculptural pieces of humans or animals that are also vessels are produced in many places, with <a href="/wiki/Moche_portrait_vessel" title="Moche portrait vessel">Moche portrait vessels</a> among the finest.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Evidence indicates an independent invention of pottery in Sub-Saharan Africa. In 2007, Swiss archaeologists discovered pieces of some of the oldest pottery in Africa at <a href="/wiki/Ounjougou" title="Ounjougou">Ounjougou</a> in Central Mali, dating back to at least 9,400 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swissinfo_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-swissinfo-5">[5]</a></sup> Excavations in the Bosumpra Cave on the Kwahu Plateau in southeastern Ghana, have revealed well-manufactured pottery decorated with channelling and impressed peigne fileté rigide dating from the early tenth-millennium cal. BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In later periods, a relationship of the introduction of pot-making in some parts of Sub-Saharan Africa with the spread of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a> has been long recognized, although the details remain controversial and awaiting further research, and no consensus has been reached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jstor.org_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_pottery" title="Ancient Egyptian pottery">Ancient Egyptian pottery</a> begins after 5,000 BC, having spread from the Levant.  There were many distinct phases of development in pottery, with very sophisticated wares being produced by the <a href="/wiki/Naqada_III" title="Naqada III">Naqada III</a> period, c. 3,200 to 3,000 BC. During the early Mediterranean civilizations of the fertile crescent, Egypt developed a non-clay-based ceramic which has come to be called <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_faience" title="Egyptian faience">Egyptian faience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[note 1]</a></sup> A similar type of body is still made in <a href="/wiki/Blue_Pottery_of_Jaipur" title="Blue Pottery of Jaipur">Jaipur</a> in India. During the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> of Islam, Egypt was a link between early centre of Islam in the Near East and Iberia which led to the impressive style of pottery.
</p><p>It is valuable to look into pottery as an archaeological record of potential interaction between peoples. When pottery is placed within the context of linguistic and migratory patterns, it becomes an even more prevalent category of social artifact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jstor.org_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-81">[81]</a></sup> As proposed by Olivier P. Gosselain, it is possible to understand ranges of cross-cultural interaction by looking closely at the <i><a href="/wiki/Cha%C3%AEne_op%C3%A9ratoire" title="Chaîne opératoire">chaîne opératoire</a></i> of ceramic production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-See_Olivier_P_2000_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-See_Olivier_P_2000-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The methods used to produce pottery in early Sub-Saharan Africa are divisible into three categories: techniques visible to the eye (decoration, firing and post-firing techniques), techniques related to the materials (selection or processing of clay, etc.), and techniques of molding or fashioning the clay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-See_Olivier_P_2000_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-See_Olivier_P_2000-83">[82]</a></sup> These three categories can be used to consider the implications of the reoccurrence of a particular sort of pottery in different areas. Generally, the techniques that are easily visible (the first category of those mentioned above) are thus readily imitated, and may indicate a more distant connection between groups, such as trade in the same market or even relatively close proximity in settlements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-See_Olivier_P_2000_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-See_Olivier_P_2000-83">[82]</a></sup> Techniques that require more studied replication (i.e., the selection of clay and the fashioning of clay) may indicate a closer connection between peoples, as these methods are usually only transmissible between potters and those otherwise directly involved in production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-See_Olivier_P_2000_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-See_Olivier_P_2000-83">[82]</a></sup> Such a relationship requires the ability of the involved parties to communicate effectively, implying pre-existing norms of contact or a shared language between the two. Thus, the patterns of technical diffusion in pot-making that are visible via archaeological findings also reveal patterns in societal interaction.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">Melanesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">Micronesia</a></b>
</p><p>Pottery has been found in archaeological sites across the islands of Oceania. It is attributed to an ancient archaeological culture called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lapita" title="Lapita">Lapita</a>. Another form of pottery called <b>Plainware</b> is found throughout sites of Oceania. The relationship between Lapita pottery and Plainware is not altogether clear.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a> never developed pottery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> After Europeans came to Australia and settled, they found deposits of clay which were analysed by English potters as excellent for making pottery. Less than 20 years later, Europeans came to Australia and began creating pottery. Since then, ceramic manufacturing, mass-produced pottery and studio pottery have flourished in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Production_issues_.28health_and_environment.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_issues_(health_and_environment)">Production issues (health and environment)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Production issues (health and environment)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="56" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="At_the_potter's_workshop.webm" height="176" id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/At_the_potter%27s_workshop.webm/220px--At_the_potter%27s_workshop.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="456392" data-framerate="25" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="600" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/26/At_the_potter%27s_workshop.webm/At_the_potter%27s_workshop.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="320904" data-framerate="25" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/26/At_the_potter%27s_workshop.webm/At_the_potter%27s_workshop.webm.240p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><track data-dir="ltr" kind="subtitles" label="română ‪(ro)‬" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AAt_the_potter%27s_workshop.webm&amp;lang=ro&amp;trackformat=vtt&amp;origin=%2A" srclang="ro" type="text/vtt"/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:At_the_potter%27s_workshop.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A potter in Maramureș County describes his materials (in Romanian and English)</div></div></div>
<p>Although many of the environmental effects of pottery production have existed for millennia, some of these have been amplified with modern technology and scales of production. The principal factors for consideration fall into two categories: (a) effects on workers, and (b) effects on the general environment.
</p><p>The chief risks on worker health include <a href="/wiki/Toxic_heavy_metal" title="Toxic heavy metal">heavy metal poisoning</a>, poor <a href="/wiki/Indoor_air_quality" title="Indoor air quality">indoor air quality</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noise_health_effects" title="Noise health effects">dangerous sound levels</a> and possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Over-illumination" title="Over-illumination">over-illumination</a>.
</p><p>Historically, "plumbism" (<a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead poisoning</a>) was a significant health concern to those glazing pottery. This was recognised at least as early as the nineteenth century, and the first legislation in the United Kingdom to limit pottery workers' exposure was introduced in 1899.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Proper ventilation to guarantee adequate indoor air quality can reduce or eliminate workers' exposure to fine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">particulate</a> matter, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, certain <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>, and crystalline <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a> (which can lead to <a href="/wiki/Silicosis" title="Silicosis">silicosis</a>). A more recent study at <a href="/wiki/Laney_College" title="Laney College">Laney College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">Oakland, California</a> suggests that all these factors can be controlled in a well-designed workshop environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>The primary environmental concerns include off-site <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>, disposal of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hazardous_materials" title="Hazardous materials">hazardous materials</a>, and fuel consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Explanatory_notes">Explanatory notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pottery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Explanatory notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The non-clay ceramic called <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_faience" title="Egyptian faience">Egyptian faience</a> should not be confused with <a href="/wiki/Faience" title="Faience">faience</a>, which is a type of glaze.</span>
